			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Verizon" redirects here. For its mobile network subsidiary, see <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Wireless" title="Verizon Wireless">Verizon Wireless</a>. For its fiber-optic residential service, see <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Fios" title="Verizon Fios">Verizon Fios</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt"> 

</p>
<p><b>Verizon Communications Inc.</b> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:GT_Verizon_Communications_Incorporated.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/GT_Verizon_Communications_Incorporated.ogg" class="internal" title="GT Verizon Communications Incorporated.ogg">listen</a></span> <small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:GT_Verizon_Communications_Incorporated.ogg" title="File:GT Verizon Communications Incorporated.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>) (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">aɪ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">ən</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">və-<span style="font-size:90%">RY</span>-zən</i></a>) is an American multinational <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications" class="mw-redirect" title="Telecommunications">telecommunications</a> conglomerate and a corporate component of the <a href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_Industrial_Average" title="Dow Jones Industrial Average">Dow Jones Industrial Average</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The company is based at <a href="/wiki/1095_Avenue_of_the_Americas" title="1095 Avenue of the Americas">1095 Avenue of the Americas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">Midtown Manhattan</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-VerizonHQ_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VerizonHQ-4">[4]</a></sup> but is <a href="/wiki/Delaware_corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Delaware corporation">incorporated</a> in <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>.

//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/22px-Increase2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300"> <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$291.73 billion</span><sup id="cite_ref-finn_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finn-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>40</span></sup> (2019)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Equity_(finance)" title="Equity (finance)">Total equity</a></span></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><img alt="Increase" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/11px-Increase2.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Increase" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/17px-Increase2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/22px-Increase2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300"> <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$62.84 billion</span><sup id="cite_ref-finn_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finn-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>80</span></sup> (2019)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Number of employees</div></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;">135,400 (2020)<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Division_(business)" title="Division (business)">Divisions</a></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_Wireless" title="Verizon Wireless">Verizon Consumer</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_Business" title="Verizon Business">Verizon Business</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_Media" title="Verizon Media">Verizon Media</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Subsidiary" title="Subsidiary">Subsidiaries</a></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_Delaware" title="Verizon Delaware">Verizon Delaware</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_Maryland" title="Verizon Maryland">Verizon Maryland</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_North" title="Verizon North">Verizon North</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_New_England" title="Verizon New England">Verizon New England</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_New_Jersey" title="Verizon New Jersey">Verizon New Jersey</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_New_York" title="Verizon New York">Verizon New York</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_Pennsylvania" title="Verizon Pennsylvania">Verizon Pennsylvania</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_South" title="Verizon South">Verizon South</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_Washington,_DC" title="Verizon Washington, DC">Verizon Washington, DC</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_Virginia" title="Verizon Virginia">Verizon Virginia</a></li>
<li>Skyward</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/XO_Communications" title="XO Communications">XO Communications</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Verizon_Hearst_Media_Partners" title="Verizon Hearst Media Partners">Verizon Hearst Media Partners</a> (50%)</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://verizon.com">verizon<wbr></wbr>.com</a></span></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height: 1.35em;"><b>Footnotes / references</b><br><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VerizonHQ_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VerizonHQ-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VZ_incomestatement_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VZ_incomestatement-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-VZ_balancesheet_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VZ_balancesheet-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-company_facts_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-company_facts-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table><p>In 1984, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">United States Department of Justice</a> mandated <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Corporation" title="AT&amp;T Corporation">AT&amp;T Corporation</a> to <a href="/wiki/Breakup_of_the_Bell_System" title="Breakup of the Bell System">break up</a> the <a href="/wiki/Bell_System" title="Bell System">Bell System</a> into seven companies, each a <a href="/wiki/Regional_Bell_Operating_Company" title="Regional Bell Operating Company">Regional Bell Operating Company</a> (RBOC), commonly referred to as "Baby Bells". One of the Baby Bells, <b>Bell Atlantic</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> came into existence in 1984, consisting of the separate operating companies <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Bell" class="mw-redirect" title="New Jersey Bell">New Jersey Bell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bell_of_Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect" title="Bell of Pennsylvania">Bell of Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diamond_State_Telephone" class="mw-redirect" title="Diamond State Telephone">Diamond State Telephone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/C%26P_Telephone" title="C&amp;P Telephone">C&amp;P Telephone</a>, with a trading area from New Jersey to Virginia. This company would later become Verizon.
</p><p>As part of a <a href="/wiki/Rebranding" title="Rebranding">rebranding</a> of the Baby Bells in the mid-1990s, all of Bell Atlantic's operating companies assumed the holding company's name. In 1997, Bell Atlantic expanded into <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> and the New England states by merging with fellow Baby Bell <a href="/wiki/NYNEX" title="NYNEX">NYNEX</a>. Bell Atlantic was the surviving company name, and the merged company moved its headquarters from Philadelphia to NYNEX's old headquarters in New York City. In 2000, Bell Atlantic acquired <a href="/wiki/GTE" title="GTE">GTE</a>, which operated telecommunications companies across most of the rest of the country that was not already in Bell Atlantic's footprint. Bell Atlantic, the surviving entity, changed its name to "Verizon", a <a href="/wiki/Portmanteau_word" class="mw-redirect" title="Portmanteau word">portmanteau</a> of <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Veritas" title="Veritas">veritas</a></i></i> (Latin for "truth") and <i><a href="/wiki/Horizon" title="Horizon">horizon</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p><p>In 2015, Verizon expanded into content ownership by acquiring <a href="/wiki/AOL" title="AOL">AOL</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and two years later it acquired <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-15">[15]</a></sup> AOL and Yahoo were amalgamated into a new division named <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Media" title="Verizon Media">Oath Inc.</a><sup id="cite_ref-:2_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup> (currently known as <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Media" title="Verizon Media">Verizon Media</a>).
</p><p>As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Verizon is one of three remaining companies that had their roots in the former Baby Bells. The other two, like Verizon, exist as a result of mergers among fellow former Baby Bell members. <a href="/wiki/SBC_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="SBC Communications">SBC Communications</a> bought the Bells' former parent AT&amp;T Corporation, and took on the <a href="/wiki/AT%26T" title="AT&amp;T">AT&amp;T</a> name. <a href="/wiki/CenturyLink" title="CenturyLink">CenturyLink</a> acquired <a href="/wiki/Qwest" title="Qwest">Qwest</a> (formerly <a href="/wiki/US_West" title="US West">US West</a>) in 2011.
</p><p>Verizon's subsidiary <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Wireless" title="Verizon Wireless">Verizon Wireless</a> is the second largest U.S. <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_wireless_communications_service_providers" title="List of United States wireless communications service providers">wireless communications</a> service provider as of April 2019, with 153.1 million mobile customers.<sup id="cite_ref-WirelessProvider_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WirelessProvider-17">[17]</a></sup> And as of 2017, Verizon is the only publicly traded telecommunications company to have two stock listings in its home country, both the NYSE (principal) and <a href="/wiki/NASDAQ" class="mw-redirect" title="NASDAQ">NASDAQ</a> (secondary).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> As of 2017, it is also the second largest telecommunications company by revenue after AT&amp;T.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Bell_Atlantic_.281983.E2.80.932000.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bell_Atlantic_(1983–2000)">Bell Atlantic (1983–2000)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Bell Atlantic (1983–2000)" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p><b>Bell Atlantic Corporation</b> was created as one of the original Regional Bell Operating Companies (RBOCs) in 1984, during the breakup of the Bell System.<sup id="cite_ref-Schofield05_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schofield05-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mayer83_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayer83-21">[21]</a></sup> Bell Atlantic's original roster of operating companies included:
</p>
<ul><li>The Bell Telephone Company of Pennsylvania<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></li>
<li>New Jersey Bell<sup id="cite_ref-Goodnough96_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodnough96-23">[23]</a></sup></li>
<li>Diamond State Telephone<sup id="cite_ref-Vise89_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vise89-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/C%26P_Telephone" title="C&amp;P Telephone">C&amp;P Telephone</a><sup id="cite_ref-Vise89_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vise89-24">[24]</a></sup> (itself comprising four subsidiaries)<sup id="cite_ref-AssociatedPress89_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AssociatedPress89-25">[25]</a></sup></li></ul><p>Bell Atlantic originally operated in the states of New Jersey, Pennsylvania, Delaware, Maryland, West Virginia, and Virginia, as well as Washington, DC.<sup id="cite_ref-Vise89_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vise89-24">[24]</a></sup></p><p>In 1996, CEO and Chairman <a href="/wiki/Raymond_W._Smith" title="Raymond W. Smith">Raymond W. Smith</a> orchestrated Bell Atlantic's merger with NYNEX.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[26]</a></sup> When it merged, it moved its corporate headquarters from Philadelphia to New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[26]</a></sup> NYNEX was consolidated into this name by 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Merger_of_equals_.282000.E2.80.932002.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Merger_of_equals_(2000–2002)">Merger of equals (2000–2002)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Merger of equals (2000–2002)" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Verizon_Logo_2000_to_2015.svg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Verizon_Logo_2000_to_2015.svg/220px-Verizon_Logo_2000_to_2015.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="429"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 127px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Verizon_Logo_2000_to_2015.svg/220px-Verizon_Logo_2000_to_2015.svg.png" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="127" data-srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Verizon_Logo_2000_to_2015.svg/330px-Verizon_Logo_2000_to_2015.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Verizon_Logo_2000_to_2015.svg/440px-Verizon_Logo_2000_to_2015.svg.png 2x" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Verizon_Logo_2000_to_2015.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Verizon logo, 2000–2015. It is still used in many locations.</div></div></div>
<p>Bell Atlantic changed its name to Verizon Communications in June 2000 when the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Communications_Commission" title="Federal Communications Commission">Federal Communications Commission</a> approved the US$64.7 billion Merger with telephone company GTE, nearly two years after the deal was proposed in July 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN00_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN00-28">[28]</a></sup> The approval came with 25 stipulations to preserve competition between local phone carriers, including investing in new markets and <a href="/wiki/Broadband" title="Broadband">broadband</a> technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN00_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN00-28">[28]</a></sup> The new entity was headed by co-CEOs Charles Lee, formerly the CEO of GTE, and Bell Atlantic CEO <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Seidenberg" title="Ivan Seidenberg">Ivan Seidenberg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN00_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN00-28">[28]</a></sup></p><p>Verizon became the largest local telephone company in the United States, operating 63 million telephone lines in 40 states.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-29">[29]</a></sup> The company also inherited 25 million mobile phone customers.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-29">[29]</a></sup> Additionally, Verizon offered internet services and long-distance calling in New York, before expanding long-distance operations to other states.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN00_28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN00-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BruceMeyerson_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BruceMeyerson-30">[30]</a></sup></p><p>The name Verizon derives from the combination of the words <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Veritas" title="Veritas">veritas</a></i></i>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for truth, and <a href="/wiki/Horizon" title="Horizon">horizon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BryanCulp_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BryanCulp-31">[31]</a></sup> The name was chosen from 8,500 candidates and the company spent $300 million on marketing the new brand.<sup id="cite_ref-BryanCulp_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BryanCulp-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JohnBorland_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JohnBorland-32">[32]</a></sup></p><p>Two months before the FCC gave final approval on the formation of Verizon Communications, Bell Atlantic formed Verizon Wireless in a <a href="/wiki/Joint_venture" title="Joint venture">joint venture</a> with the British telecommunications company <a href="/wiki/Vodafone" title="Vodafone">Vodafone</a> in April 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-JohnBorland_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JohnBorland-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN99_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN99-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ErichLuening_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ErichLuening-34">[34]</a></sup> The companies established Verizon Wireless as its own business operated by Bell Atlantic, which owned 55% of the venture.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN99_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN99-33">[33]</a></sup> Vodafone retained 45% of the company.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN99_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN99-33">[33]</a></sup> The deal was valued at approximately $70 billion and created a mobile carrier with 23 million customers.<sup id="cite_ref-JohnBorland_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JohnBorland-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN99_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN99-33">[33]</a></sup> Verizon Wireless merged Bell Atlantic's wireless network, Vodafone's AirTouch and <a href="/wiki/PrimeCo" title="PrimeCo">PrimeCo</a> holdings, and the wireless division of GTE.<sup id="cite_ref-CNN99_33-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN99-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ToddWeiss_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ToddWeiss-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EveTahmincioglu_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EveTahmincioglu-36">[36]</a></sup> Due to its size, Verizon Wireless was able to offer national coverage at competitive rates, giving it an advantage over regional providers typical of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-JohnBorland_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JohnBorland-32">[32]</a></sup></p><p>During its first operational year, Verizon Wireless released <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Web" class="mw-redirect" title="Mobile Web">Mobile Web</a>, an Internet service that allowed customers to access partner sites such as <a href="/wiki/E*Trade" class="mw-redirect" title="E*Trade">E*Trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/ABC_News" title="ABC News">ABC News</a>, <a href="/wiki/ESPN" title="ESPN">ESPN</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amazon.com" class="mw-redirect" title="Amazon.com">Amazon.com</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ticketmaster" title="Ticketmaster">Ticketmaster</a> and <a href="/wiki/MSN" title="MSN">MSN</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ErichLuening_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ErichLuening-34">[34]</a></sup> as well as the "New Every Two" program, which gave customers a free phone with every two-year service contract.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> In another partnership with MSN in 2002, Verizon Wireless launched the mobile content service "VZW with MSN" and a phone that utilized the <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USAT02_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAT02-38">[38]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2000, approximately 85,000 Verizon workers went on an <a href="/wiki/Verizon_strike_of_2000" title="Verizon strike of 2000">18-day labor strike</a> after their <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">union</a> contracts expired.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNNAug00_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNAug00-40">[40]</a></sup> The strike affected quarterly revenues,<sup id="cite_ref-CecilyBarnes_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CecilyBarnes-41">[41]</a></sup> resulting in Verizon Wireless' postponement of the company's <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">IPO</a><sup id="cite_ref-CecilyBarnes_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CecilyBarnes-41">[41]</a></sup> (the IPO was ultimately cancelled in 2003, because the company no longer needed to raise revenue for Verizon Wireless due to increased profits<sup id="cite_ref-Telegeography_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegeography-42">[42]</a></sup>), and created a backlog of repairs.<sup id="cite_ref-CNNAug00_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNAug00-40">[40]</a></sup> This strike did not involve all company employees as mostly line technicians and user technicians of the company are union.
</p><p>Verizon launched <a href="/wiki/3G" title="3G">3G</a> service in 2002, which doubled the Internet speeds of the time to 144kb a second.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In August 2002, Verizon began offering local, long-distance, and mobile calling, as well as Internet service, in a <a href="/wiki/Product_bundling" title="Product bundling">bundle</a>. It was initially only available to customers in <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BruceMeyerson_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BruceMeyerson-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="2003.E2.80.932005"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2003–2005">2003–2005</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 2003–2005" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In June 2003, Verizon Wireless backed an FCC-issued portability requirement that permitted consumers to take their phone numbers with them across carriers.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The company gained 1.5 million new subscribers the following quarter, partially due to the rule change.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The following year, in April 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_Industrial_Average" title="Dow Jones Industrial Average">Dow Jones Industrial Average</a> added Verizon Communications to its <a href="/wiki/Stock_market_index" title="Stock market index">stock market index</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ChrisIsidore_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisIsidore-46">[46]</a></sup> Verizon replaced telecom competitor AT&amp;T, which had been a part of the index since the Great Depression.<sup id="cite_ref-ChrisIsidore_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChrisIsidore-46">[46]</a></sup></p><p>On December 22, 2004, <a href="/wiki/Mail_server" class="mw-redirect" title="Mail server">mail servers</a> at Verizon.net were configured not to accept connections from Europe, by default, in an attempt to reduce <a href="/wiki/Spam_email" class="mw-redirect" title="Spam email">spam email</a> that was originating from the region. Individual domains would only be unblocked upon request.<sup id="cite_ref-JohnLeyden_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JohnLeyden-47">[47]</a></sup></p><p>In 2004, Verizon launched its <a href="/wiki/Verizon_FiOS" class="mw-redirect" title="Verizon FiOS">Fios</a> Internet service, which transmits data over <a href="/wiki/Optical_fiber" title="Optical fiber">fiber optic</a> cables, in <a href="/wiki/Keller,_Texas" title="Keller, Texas">Keller, Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PeterSvensson_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PeterSvensson-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Charny04_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charny04-49">[49]</a></sup> The company launched Fios TV in September 2005, also in Keller, Texas. Twenty percent of qualified homes signed up by the end of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-BartonEckert_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BartonEckert-50">[50]</a></sup> By January 2006, Fios offered over 350 channels in eight states, including 20 <a href="/wiki/High-definition_television" title="High-definition television">high-definition television</a> channels as well as <a href="/wiki/Video_on_demand" title="Video on demand">video on demand</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BartonEckert_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BartonEckert-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="MCI_acquisition">MCI acquisition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: MCI acquisition" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Verizon began negotiations to purchase long-distance carrier <a href="/wiki/MCI_Inc." title="MCI Inc.">MCI</a> in 2005. MCI accepted the company's initial $6.75 billion offer in February 2005, but then received a higher offer from <a href="/wiki/Qwest_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Qwest Communications">Qwest Communications</a>. Verizon increased its bid to $7.6 billion (or $23.50 a share), which MCI accepted on March 29, 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-PaulLaMonica_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaulLaMonica-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mergers_and_acquisitions" title="Mergers and acquisitions">acquisition</a> gave the company access to MCI's one million corporate clients and international holdings, expanding Verizon's presence into global markets.<sup id="cite_ref-PaulLaMonica_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PaulLaMonica-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DavidEwalt_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidEwalt-52">[52]</a></sup> As a result, Verizon Business was established as a new division to serve the company's business and government customers.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The FCC approved the deal on November 5, 2005, valuing it at $8.5 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Verizon's 2006 revenues rose by as much as 20% following the purchase.<sup id="cite_ref-CraytonHarrison_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CraytonHarrison-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="2006.E2.80.932010"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2006–2010">2006–2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2006–2010" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In May 2006, <i><a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a></i> reported that Verizon, as well as AT&amp;T and <a href="/wiki/BellSouth" title="BellSouth">BellSouth</a>, had given the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Agency" title="National Security Agency">National Security Agency</a> <a href="/wiki/Landline" title="Landline">landline</a> phone records following the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MelissaMcNamara_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MelissaMcNamara-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN06_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN06-57">[57]</a></sup> That same month, a $50 billion lawsuit was filed by two lawyers on behalf of all Verizon subscribers for privacy violations and to prevent the company from releasing additional records without consent or warrant.<sup id="cite_ref-MelissaMcNamara_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MelissaMcNamara-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN06_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNN06-57">[57]</a></sup> Protesters staged the National Day of Out(R)age due in part to the controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-DeclanMcCullagh_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeclanMcCullagh-58">[58]</a></sup> Verizon stated in 2007 that the company fulfilled only "lawful demands" for information,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> though also acknowledged surrendering customer information to government agencies without court orders or <a href="/wiki/Warrant_(law)" title="Warrant (law)">warrants</a> 720 times between 2005 and 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-EllenNakashima_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EllenNakashima-60">[60]</a></sup></p><p>Verizon won a lawsuit against <a href="/wiki/Vonage" title="Vonage">Vonage</a> in March 2007 for <a href="/wiki/Patent_infringement" title="Patent infringement">patent infringement</a>. The three patents named were filed by Bell Atlantic in 1997 and relate to the conversion of IP addresses into phone numbers, a key technology of Vonage's business.<sup id="cite_ref-LarryBarrett_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LarryBarrett-61">[61]</a></sup> The company was awarded US$58 million in damages and future royalties.<sup id="cite_ref-LarryBarrett_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LarryBarrett-61">[61]</a></sup> Vonage later lost an appeal and was ordered to pay Verizon $120 million.<sup id="cite_ref-JeffStOnge_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JeffStOnge-62">[62]</a></sup></p><p>In May 2007, Verizon acquired Cybertrust, a privately held provider of global information security services.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p><p>Verizon Wireless reversed a controversial decision in September 2007 to deny <a href="/wiki/NARAL_Pro-Choice_America" title="NARAL Pro-Choice America">NARAL Pro-Choice America</a> a short code through which the organization could <a href="/wiki/Text_messaging" title="Text messaging">text</a> consumers who had signed up for messaging from the group. They had initially refused the group access to a code by reserving the right to block "controversial or unsavory" messages.<sup id="cite_ref-AdamLiptak_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AdamLiptak-64">[64]</a></sup></p><p>In November 2007, Verizon opened its networks for the first time to third party <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">apps</a> and devices,<sup id="cite_ref-BryanGardiner07_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BryanGardiner07-65">[65]</a></sup> a decision that allowed it to participate in the FCC's <a href="/wiki/United_States_2008_wireless_spectrum_auction" class="mw-redirect" title="United States 2008 wireless spectrum auction">2008 700 MHz auction of "open access" spectrum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BryanGardiner07_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BryanGardiner07-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PeterKaplan_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PeterKaplan-66">[66]</a></sup> During that auction, the company bid $9.4 billion and won the bulk of national and local licenses for airwaves reaching approximately 469 million people.<sup id="cite_ref-PeterKaplan_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PeterKaplan-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BryanGardiner08_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BryanGardiner08-67">[67]</a></sup> Verizon utilized the increased spectrum for its 4G service.<sup id="cite_ref-PeterKaplan_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PeterKaplan-66">[66]</a></sup></p><p>Verizon Wireless purchased wireless carrier <a href="/wiki/Alltel" title="Alltel">Alltel</a> for $28.1 billion in June 2008. The acquisition included 13 million customers, which allowed Verizon Wireless to surpass AT&amp;T in number of customers and reach new markets in rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-SineadCarew_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SineadCarew-68">[68]</a></sup></p><p>In October 2010, Verizon Wireless paid $77.8 million in refunds and FCC penalties for overcharging 15 million customers for data services. The company stated the overcharges were accidental and only amounted to a few dollars per customer.<sup id="cite_ref-ScottWoolley_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ScottWoolley-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CeciliaKang_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CeciliaKang-70">[70]</a></sup></p><p>On February 4, 2010, <a href="/wiki/4chan" title="4chan">4chan</a> started receiving reports from Verizon Wireless customers that they were having difficulties accessing the site's image boards. 4chan administrators found that only traffic on port 80 to the boards.4chan.org domain was affected, leading them to believe that the block was intentional. On February 7, 2010, Verizon Wireless confirmed that 4chan.org was "explicitly blocked"<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> after Verizon's security and external experts detected sweep attacks coming from an IP address associated with the 4chan network. Traffic was restored several days later.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2010, the chairmen of Verizon and Google agreed that <a href="/wiki/Network_neutrality_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Network neutrality in the United States">network neutrality</a> should be defined and limited.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p><p>Verizon introduced its <a href="/wiki/4G" title="4G">4G</a> <a href="/wiki/LTE_(telecommunication)" title="LTE (telecommunication)">LTE</a> network in 38 markets in December 2010, as well as in airports in seven additional cities. The company planned on a three-year continuous expansion of the 4G service.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Selling_wirelines_.282005.E2.80.932010_.26_2015.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Selling_wirelines_(2005–2010_&amp;_2015)">Selling wirelines (2005–2010 &amp; 2015)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Selling wirelines (2005–2010 &amp; 2015)" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Between 2005 and 2010, Verizon divested wireline operations in several states to Frontier in order to focus on its wireless, Fios internet and Fios TV businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-CraytonHarrison_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CraytonHarrison-55">[55]</a></sup> It sold 700,000 lines in Hawaii in 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-CraytonHarrison_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CraytonHarrison-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PeterSayer_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PeterSayer-76">[76]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Corporate_spin-off" title="Corporate spin-off">spun off</a> lines in Maine, New Hampshire and Vermont in January 2007 that were then purchased by <a href="/wiki/FairPoint_Communications" title="FairPoint Communications">FairPoint Communications</a> for $2.72 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-CraytonHarrison_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CraytonHarrison-55">[55]</a></sup> Verizon also shed its telephone directory business in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-RyanFuhrmann_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RyanFuhrmann-77">[77]</a></sup></p><p>In May 2009, the company spun off wirelines in Arizona, Idaho, Illinois, Indiana, Michigan, Nevada, North Carolina, Ohio, Oregon, South Carolina, Washington, West Virginia, and Wisconsin into a company that then merged with <a href="/wiki/Frontier_Communications" title="Frontier Communications">Frontier Communications</a> in a deal valued at $8.6 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> In 2016, Verizon sold its wireline operations in Texas, Florida, and California to Frontier.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="2011.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2011–present">2011–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2011–present" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On January 27, 2011, Verizon acquired <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Enterprise_Solutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Verizon Enterprise Solutions">Terremark</a>, an information technology services company for $1.4 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Ivan Seidenberg retired as Verizon's CEO on August 1, 2011. <a href="/wiki/Lowell_McAdam" title="Lowell McAdam">Lowell McAdam</a> succeeded him.<sup id="cite_ref-PeterSvensson11_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PeterSvensson11-82">[82]</a></sup></p><p>In December 2011, the non-partisan organization <a href="/wiki/Public_Campaign#Reports" class="mw-redirect" title="Public Campaign">Public Campaign</a> criticized Verizon for its <a href="/wiki/Tax_avoidance" title="Tax avoidance">tax avoidance</a> procedures after it spent $52.34 million on <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbying</a> while collecting $951 million in tax rebates between 2008 and 2010 and making a profit of $32.5 billion. The same report also criticized Verizon for increasing executive pay by 167% in 2010 for its top five executives while laying off 21,308 workers between 2008 and 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-AshleyPortero_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AshleyPortero-83">[83]</a></sup> However, in its Form 10-K filed with the SEC on February 24, 2012, Verizon reported having paid more than $11.1 billion in taxes (including income, employment and property taxes) from 2009 to 2011. In addition, the company reported in the 10-K that most of the drop in employment since 2008 was due to a voluntary retirement offer.<sup id="cite_ref-SEC_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEC-84">[84]</a></sup></p><p>In June 2012, as part of its strategy to expand into new growth areas in its wireless business, Verizon purchased Hughes <a href="/wiki/Telematics" title="Telematics">Telematics</a>—a company that produces wireless features for <a href="/wiki/Automobiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobiles">automobiles</a>—for $612 million.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Also in June 2012, Verizon's <a href="/wiki/Enhanced_911" class="mw-redirect" title="Enhanced 911">E-911</a> service failed in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/June_2012_North_American_derecho" title="June 2012 North American derecho">June 2012 derecho storm</a> in several northern Virginia suburbs of Washington, D.C., with some problems lasting several days.<sup id="cite_ref-JustinJuvenal_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JustinJuvenal-86">[86]</a></sup> The FCC conducted an investigation<sup id="cite_ref-JustinJuvenal_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JustinJuvenal-86">[86]</a></sup> and in January 2013 released a report detailing the problems that led to the failure. Verizon reported that it had already addressed or was addressing a number of the issues related to the FCC report, including the causes of generator failures, conducting audits of backup systems and making its monitoring systems less centralized,<sup id="cite_ref-EdwardWyatt_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EdwardWyatt-87">[87]</a></sup> although the FCC indicated that Verizon still needed to make additional improvements.<sup id="cite_ref-MaryFlaherty_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MaryFlaherty-88">[88]</a></sup></p><p>In July 2012, the FCC ruled that Verizon must stop charging users an added fee for using 4G smartphones and tablets as <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi_hotspot" class="mw-redirect" title="Wi-Fi hotspot">Wi-Fi hotspots</a> (known as "<a href="/wiki/Tethering" title="Tethering">tethering</a>"). Verizon had been charging its customers, even those with "unlimited" plans, $20 per month for tethering. As part of the settlement, Verizon made a voluntary payment of $1.25 million to the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Treasury" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Treasury">U.S. Treasury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaughan-Nichols_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaughan-Nichols-89">[89]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2012, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Department of Justice</a> approved Verizon's purchase of <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Wireless_Services" title="Advanced Wireless Services">Advanced Wireless Services</a> (AWS) spectrum from a consortium of cable companies, including <a href="/wiki/Comcast" title="Comcast">Comcast</a>, <a href="/wiki/Time_Warner_Cable" title="Time Warner Cable">Time Warner Cable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bright_House_Networks" title="Bright House Networks">Bright House Networks</a>, for $3.9 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-KevinFitchard_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KevinFitchard-90">[90]</a></sup> Verizon began expanding its LTE network utilizing these extra airwaves in October 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p><p>On June 5, 2013, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reported it had obtained an order by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> (FBI) and approved by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Foreign_Intelligence_Surveillance_Court" title="United States Foreign Intelligence Surveillance Court">United States Foreign Intelligence Surveillance Court</a> that required Verizon to provide the NSA with telephone <a href="/wiki/Metadata" title="Metadata">metadata</a> for all calls originating in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-EwenMacAskill_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EwenMacAskill-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FoxNews_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FoxNews-93">[93]</a></sup> Verizon Wireless was not part of the NSA data collection for wireless accounts due to foreign ownership issues.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> (see also <a href="/wiki/MAINWAY" title="MAINWAY">MAINWAY</a> article)
</p><p>In September 2013, Verizon purchased the 45% stake in Verizon Wireless owned by Vodafone for $130 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_News_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-95">[95]</a></sup> The deal closed on February 21, 2014, becoming the third largest corporate deal ever signed, giving Verizon Communications sole ownership of Verizon Wireless.<sup id="cite_ref-DevindraHardawar_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DevindraHardawar-96">[96]</a></sup></p><p>On January 14, 2014, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_District_of_Columbia_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit">DC Circuit Court of Appeals</a> struck down the FCC's <a href="/wiki/Net_neutrality" title="Net neutrality">net neutrality</a> rules after Verizon filed suit against them in January 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-LeticiaMiranda_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LeticiaMiranda-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RyanSingel_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RyanSingel-98">[98]</a></sup> In June 2016, in a 184-page ruling, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_District_of_Columbia_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit">United States Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit</a> upheld, by a 2–1 vote, the FCC's net neutrality rules and the FCC's determination that broadband access is a <a href="/wiki/Public_utility" title="Public utility">public utility</a>, rather than a luxury. AT&amp;T and the telecom industry said that they would seek to appeal the decision to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p><p>On January 22, 2014, <i>The Wall Street Journal</i> reported that Verizon received more than 1,000 requests for information about its subscribers on national security grounds via <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Letters" class="mw-redirect" title="National Security Letters">National Security Letters</a>. In total, Verizon received 321,545 requests from federal, state and local law enforcement for U.S. customer information.<sup id="cite_ref-RyanKnutson_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RyanKnutson-100">[100]</a></sup> In May 2015, Verizon agreed to pay $90 million "to settle federal and state investigations into allegations mobile customers were improperly billed for premium text messages."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p><p>In late October 2014, Verizon Wireless launched <i>SugarString</i>, a technology news website. The publication attracted controversy after it was reported that its writers were forbidden from publishing articles related to <a href="/wiki/Net_neutrality" title="Net neutrality">net neutrality</a> or domestic <a href="/wiki/Surveillance" title="Surveillance">surveillance</a>. Although Verizon denied that this was the case, the site (described as being a pilot project) was shuttered in December.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p><p>In August 2015, Verizon launched <a href="/wiki/Verizon_Hum" title="Verizon Hum">Hum</a>, a service and device offering vehicle diagnostic and monitoring tools for vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> On August 1, 2016, Verizon announced its acquisition of <a href="/wiki/Fleetmatics" title="Fleetmatics">Fleetmatics</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Fleet_telematics_system" title="Fleet telematics system">fleet telematics system</a> company in <a href="/wiki/Dublin,_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Dublin, Ireland">Dublin, Ireland</a>, for $2.4 billion, to build products that it offers to enterprises for logistics and mobile workforces.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> On September 12, 2016, Verizon announced its acquisition of Sensity, a startup for <a href="/wiki/Intelligent_street_lighting" title="Intelligent street lighting">LED sensors</a>, in an effort to bolster its <a href="/wiki/Internet_of_things" title="Internet of things">IoT</a> portfolio.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p><p>In October 2016, Verizon was accused by <a href="/wiki/Communications_Workers_of_America" title="Communications Workers of America">Communications Workers of America</a> of deliberately refusing to maintain its <a href="/wiki/Plain_old_telephone_service" title="Plain old telephone service">copper telephone service</a>. The organization released internal memos and other documents stating that Verizon workers in Pennsylvania were being instructed to, in areas with network problems, migrate voice-only customers to VoiceLink—a system that delivers telephone service over the Verizon Wireless network, and not to repair the copper lines. VoiceLink has limitations, including incompatibility with services or devices that require the transmission of data over the telephone line, and a dependency on a battery backup in case of power failure. The memo warned that technicians who do not follow this procedure would be subject to "disciplinary action up to and including dismissal". A Verizon spokesperson responded to the allegations, stating that the company's top priority was to restore service to customers as quickly as possible, and that VoiceLink was a means of doing so in the event that larger repairs have to be done to the infrastructure. The spokesperson stated that it was "hard to argue with disciplining someone who intentionally leaves a customer without service".<sup id="cite_ref-ars-verizoncopperfired_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ars-verizoncopperfired-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ars-voicelink_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ars-voicelink-108">[108]</a></sup></p><p>In November 2016, Verizon acquired mapping startup SocialRadar; its technology will be integrated with <a href="/wiki/MapQuest" title="MapQuest">MapQuest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p><p>On January 26, 2017, <i>The Washington Post</i> reported that Verizon was in talks to merge with <a href="/wiki/Charter_Communications" title="Charter Communications">Charter Communications</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p><p>In 2017, Verizon partnered with <a href="/wiki/Alley_(company)" title="Alley (company)">Alley</a> to develop a number of <a href="/wiki/Coworking" title="Coworking">coworking</a> spaces under the name "Alley powered by Verizon".<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p><p>On March 13, 2017, Verizon was sued by New York City for violating its cable franchise agreement, which required the provider to pass a fiberoptic network to all households in the city by June 30, 2014. Verizon disputed the claims, citing landlords not granting permission to install the equipment on their properties, and an understanding with the government that the fiber network would follow the same routes as its copper lines, and did not necessarily mean it would have to pass the lines in front of every property.<sup id="cite_ref-ars-verizonnyclawsuit_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ars-verizonnyclawsuit-114">[114]</a></sup></p><p>On April 27, 2017, Verizon invested $10 million in Renovo Auto, a Campbell, California-based autonomous vehicle company.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p><p><span id="Verizon_Connect">Verizon Connect</span> was created in 2018, combining the individual units Telematics, Fleetmatics, and Telogis.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moritz18_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moritz18-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Szal18_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Szal18-118">[118]</a></sup></p><p>On Jan 17, 2019, Verizon announced that it would offer anti-spam and robocalling features free of charge to all its customers from March.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p><p>Verizon began rolling out its <a href="/wiki/5G" title="5G">5G</a> mobile network in April 2019 and by year's end, it was active in 30 cities.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> Unlike other U.S. carriers, Verizon only uses <a href="/wiki/Extremely_high_frequency" title="Extremely high frequency">millimeter-wave</a> (mmWave) spectrum for its 5G network.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> While capable of very high speeds, mmWave has limited range and poor building penetration.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Acquisition_of_AOL_and_Yahoo">Acquisition of AOL and Yahoo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Acquisition of AOL and Yahoo" data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>On May 12, 2015, Verizon announced they would acquire <a href="/wiki/AOL" title="AOL">AOL</a> at $50 per share, for a deal valued around $4.4 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> The following year, Verizon announced that it would acquire the core internet business of <a href="/wiki/Yahoo" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo">Yahoo</a> for $4.83 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Following the completion of the acquisitions, Verizon created a new division called Oath, which includes the AOL and Yahoo brands.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup> The sale did not include Yahoo's stakes in <a href="/wiki/Alibaba_Group" title="Alibaba Group">Alibaba Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!_Japan" title="Yahoo! Japan">Yahoo! Japan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p><p>On March 16, 2017, Verizon announced that it would discontinue the e-mail services provided for its internet subscribers, and migrate them to <a href="/wiki/AOL_Mail" title="AOL Mail">AOL Mail</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mcn-aolverizonmail_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcn-aolverizonmail-132">[132]</a></sup></p><p>On May 23, 2017, Verizon CEO Lowell McAdam confirmed the company's plan to launch a streaming TV service.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The integrated AOL-Yahoo operation, housed under the newly created Oath division, will be organized around key content-based pillars.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p><p>On June 13, 2017, Verizon completed its acquisition of Yahoo for $4.48 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-Merger_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Merger-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dodge_Ram_Van_Verizon.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Dodge_Ram_Van_Verizon.jpg/200px-Dodge_Ram_Van_Verizon.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="149" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="761"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 200px;height: 149px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Dodge_Ram_Van_Verizon.jpg/200px-Dodge_Ram_Van_Verizon.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="200" data-height="149" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dodge_Ram_Van_Verizon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Verizon service van</div></div></div>
<p>On December 10, 2018, Verizon announced that 10,400 managers had agreed to leave the company as part of a "voluntary separation program" that was offered to 44,000 employees, resulting in a cut to around 7% of its workforce. At the same time, the company announced a $4.6 billion write-off on its media division, citing "increased competitive and market pressures throughout 2018 that have resulted in lower-than-expected revenues and earning."<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Verizon_Communications&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Finances" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<p>For the fiscal year 2019, Verizon reported earnings of US$19.265 billion, with an annual revenue of US$131.868 billion, an increase of 0.77% over the previous fiscal cycle. Verizon's shares traded at over $45 per share, and its market capitalization was valued at over US$229.1 billion in October 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> As of 2018, Verizon is ranked #16 on the <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500">Fortune 500</a> rankings of the largest United States corporations by total revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
